\begin{itemize}

\]

We need a rigorous analysis of the game. Let's define the game state more abstractly.

We have two sequences: x_n. The constraints are:
- After each odd n: S_n ≤ λ n.
- After each even n: Q_n ≤ n.

Players choose numbers. The game ends when at the start of a turn, the constraint for that turn is impossible to satisfy (i.e., the cumulative sum or sum of squares already exceeds the bound). So if at odd n, S_{n-1} > λ n, Alice loses; if at even n, Q_{n-1} > n, Bazza loses.

Thus we can think of the game as two players trying to push the opponent's cumulative measure beyond a bound. The key is the relationship between S and Q via Cauchy-Schwarz: Q_n ≥ S_n^2 / n. So if Bazza wants to make Q_n exceed n (to force Alice to lose at odd n+1?), actually Bazza loses if Q_{n-1} > n at even n. So Alice wants to make Q_{n-1} > n. But Q_{n-1} ≥ S_{n-1}^2 / (n-1). So if S_{n-1} is large enough, then Q_{n-1} might exceed n. But that's the condition for Alice to win: at an odd n, after her move, Q_n > n+1? Actually careful: Alice wins if at some even n, Q_{n-1} > n. Since she chooses x_n on odd n, after her move, Q_n is increased. Then at even n+1, Bazza must have Q_n ≤ n+1. So if Q_n > n+1, Bazza loses. So Alice wants to make Q_n > n+1 at an odd n. Since Q_n = Q_{n-1} + x_n^2, and she can choose x_n up to λ n - S_{n-1}. So she wants to maximize x_n^2 given S_{n-1} and the bound. So the condition for her to be able to win on her turn is: there exists x_n ≥ 0 such that x_n ≤ λ n - S_{n-1} and Q_{n-1} + x_n^2 > n+1. Since she can choose x_n, she can achieve that if and only if Q_{n-1} + (λ n - S_{n-1})^2 > n+1, provided λ n - S_{n-1} ≥ 0. If λ n - S_{n-1} < 0, she cannot even move. So Alice has a winning strategy if she can force the state to such that at some odd n, Q_{n-1} + (λ n - S_{n-1})^2 > n+1 and also S_{n-1} ≤ λ n. But note: if she can achieve that, she can win by choosing x_n = λ n - S_{n-1} (or maybe a slightly smaller if that makes the square exceed the bound? Actually she wants to make Q_n > n+1, so she can pick the maximum possible x_n to maximize x_n^2. So if that maximum yields Q_n > n+1, she wins. So the condition for her to be able to win on her turn is: Q_{n-1} + (λ n - S_{n-1})^2 > n+1. But she also must have S_{n-1} ≤ λ n to be able to move (otherwise she loses immediately). So if the state (S_{n-1}, Q_{n-1}) satisfies S_{n-1} ≤ λ n and Q_{n-1} + (λ n - S_{n-1})^2 > n+1, then Alice can win by playing x_n = λ n - S_{n-1}. However, note that she could also play a smaller x_n; but the maximum gives the best chance. So she will win if she can achieve that condition.

Similarly, Bazza wants to make S_{n-1} > λ n at an even n. Since after his move at even n, S_n = S_{n-1} + x_n. He can choose x_n up to sqrt(n - Q_{n-1}). He wants to make S_n > λ (n+1)? Actually he wants at the next odd turn n+1, Alice to lose: S_n > λ (n+1). Since he chooses x_n, he can increase S. So his winning condition at even n is: there exists x_n ≥ 0 such that x_n ≤ sqrt(n - Q_{n-1}) and S_{n-1} + x_n > λ (n+1). Since he can choose x_n, he can achieve that if and only if S_{n-1} + sqrt(n - Q_{n-1}) > λ (n+1), provided sqrt(n - Q_{n-1}) ≥ 0. Also, he must have Q_{n-1} ≤ n to be able to move (otherwise he loses immediately). So if the state (S_{n-1}, Q_{n-1}) satisfies Q_{n-1} ≤ n and S_{n-1} + sqrt(n - Q_{n-1}) > λ (n+1), then Bazza can win by playing x_n = sqrt(n - Q_{n-1}) (or maybe a smaller if that makes the sum exceed the bound? Actually he wants to maximize S, so he picks the maximum possible x_n). So if that condition holds, Bazza can win.

Thus the game is about the players trying to steer the state to satisfy their winning condition. The game could also be drawn if the players can avoid ever satisfying the opponent's winning condition. But note that if one player can force a win, they will eventually do so.

We can try to find the "critical" region in the (S, Q) plane for the game. The initial state: after 0 moves, S_0=0, Q_0=0. The first turn is odd (n=1). Alice must choose x_1 such that S_1 ≤ λ*1. She can pick any x_1 in [0, λ]. So after her move, state (S_1, Q_1) = (x_1, x_1^2). She might want to set x_1 to something that gives her a chance to win later. For her to win, she needs at some later odd n, Q_{n-1} + (λ n - S_{n-1})^2 > n+1. Since she can control x_1, she can try to set up the state.

For Bazza to win, he needs at some even n, S_{n-1} + sqrt(n - Q_{n-1}) > λ (n+1). He can choose x_n.

We need to determine for which λ Alice has a winning strategy. That is, can she force the state to satisfy her winning condition before Bazza can force his? Since both players are rational, we can analyze the game as a kind of "reachability" game.

Let’s try to find a threshold λ0 such that:
- If λ > λ0, Alice can force a win.
- If λ < λ0, Bazza can force a win.
- At λ = λ0, maybe draw or one wins? Typically such problems have a threshold where one player can force a win for larger λ, and the other for smaller λ, and at the threshold perhaps the game is a draw (both can avoid losing indefinitely) or maybe one still wins. We need to find all λ for which Alice has a winning strategy, and all those for which Bazza has a winning strategy. Possibly there is a range for each, with a gap (draw) or overlap? Let's try to analyze the game more abstractly using inequalities. For any sequence, we have S_n^2 ≤ n Q_n. So at odd n, after Alice's move, we have S_n ≤ λ n, and Q_n = Q_{n-1} + x_n^2. Similarly, at even n, after Bazza's move, we have Q_n ≤ n, and S_n = S_{n-1} + x_n.

We can try to bound the sum S_n after many moves. Consider the product of S and Q? Or we can think of the "value" of the game as the supremum of the sum that can be forced.

Let’s define the "worst-case" for Alice if she wants to avoid losing. She might try to keep the sum as low as possible. But Bazza wants to increase the sum. So the threshold λ is the maximum rate at which Bazza can force the sum to grow, relative to the index.

Thus the game can be seen as: at each step, the player can choose a number, and the state evolves. We can try to find a "strategy" for one player that forces the opponent into a losing condition.

Given the complexity, maybe we can solve the problem by finding the "critical" λ where the two players have a "strategy" that guarantees that the opponent cannot avoid losing. This is similar to a "pursuit" game where the players try to maintain a certain inequality.

Let's try to analyze the game by "scaling" the constraints. Define the "value" of the game in terms of the maximum possible sum after many moves. Suppose we consider the "optimal" play for Bazza to maximize S_n. He can choose x_{2k} as large as possible given Q_{2k-1}. Alice can choose x_{2k-1} to minimize S_{2k-1} or Q_{2k-1}. So she needs to decide.

Consider the possibility that both players use "maximal" strategies: Alice always picks x_{odd} = λ n - S_{n-1} (max allowed), and Bazza always picks x_{even} = sqrt(n - Q_{n-1}) (max allowed). Let’s denote n odd: n=2k+1. After Alice's move, S_{2k+1} = λ (2k+1) (since she uses max). And Q_{2k+1} = Q_{2k} + (λ (2k+1) - S_{2k})^2. But S_{2k} is the sum after Bazza's move at n=2k. We can try to see if Alice can ever have Q_{2k+1} > 2k+2. Since Q_{2k+1} = Q_{2k} + (λ(2k+1) - S_{2k})^2 = 2k + (λ(2k+1) - S_{2k})^2. Let's attempt to find a "potential" function that is monotonic and gives a threshold. Consider the inequality S_n^2 ≤ n Q_n. If at even step n, Q_n ≤ n, then S_n ≤ n. That's one bound. For Alice's win, she needs Q_n > n+1 at odd n. For Bazza's win, he needs S_n > λ n at odd n.

It seems the process continues with Q always being just below the next even bound. The question is whether she can ever make Q exceed the next even bound. The recurrence for d_k = λ(2k-1) - S_{2k-2} (the difference at odd steps) seems to be: d_{k+1} = 2λ - sqrt(2 - d_k^2). For λ=0.85, we have fixed points at d = λ ± sqrt(1-λ^2) = 0.85 ± sqrt(1-0.7225)=0.85±0.5277=1.3777 or 0.3223. The sequence of d_k (starting with d_1=0.85) seems to converge to 0.3223. So d_k becomes about 0.322, which is positive. Then the allowed x at odd steps is d_k, which is about 0.322. So she picks that, and Q increases by d_k^2. So Q_{2k+1} = 2k + d_k^2. Since d_k ≈ 0.322, d_k^2 ≈ 0.1035, so Q_{2k+1} = 2k + 0.1035. The next even bound is 2k+2. So Q_{2k+1} is far less than 2k+2. So she cannot win by picking the maximum. She could choose a smaller x, but that would make Q increase even less. So she cannot win. In the above, we saw occasional larger allowed x at odd steps (like at n=71, allowed 1.324) but that's because S was close to the bound? Actually that was due to a different pattern: after many steps, the sum S becomes close to λ n? In the play, we had S_70=59.026, Q_70=70.0, then at n=71, allowed x_71 = 60.35 - 59.026 = 1.324, so indeed the allowed x was large because S was below λ*71 by about 1.324. That large allowed x allowed her to make Q_71 = 70 + 1.753 = 71.753, which is still less than 72. So she didn't win. Then at n=73, allowed x_73 = 62.05 - 61.726 = 0.324, back to small. So the large allowed x occurred when S was low relative to λ n, but then after she picks that large x, S becomes λ n (or close) and Q increases by a lot, but not enough to exceed the bound. Then the process continues. So it seems that for λ=0.85, the game may be a draw as well. But we need to check if there is a strategy for Alice to force a win. The above play used the maximal strategy at each step; it's not necessarily optimal for her. She might try a different strategy to build up Q more quickly. For instance, she could choose x_1 not at max, to give herself a larger x_3? She could try to pick x_3 larger than the allowed? No, she can't exceed the bound. So the only flexibility is to choose a smaller x at odd steps, which would keep Q low, and maybe allow her to pick a larger x later? But if she picks a smaller x, that reduces the increase in S, which might give her a larger allowed x at the next odd step? Maybe the threshold is λ = √2/2 ≈ 0.7071? For λ < 0.7071, Bazza wins; for λ > 0.7071, Alice wins? But we saw that for λ=0.85, Alice didn't seem to win. Let's test λ=0.75 with the maximal strategy. We are asked: "Alice and Bazza are playing the inekoalaty game, a two-player game whose rules depend on a positive real number λ which is known to both players. On the nth turn of the game (starting with n=1) the following happens:
- If n is odd, Alice chooses a nonnegative real number x_n such that x_1 + x_2 + ... + x_n ≤ λ n.
- If n is even, Bazza chooses a nonnegative real number x_n such that x_1^2 + x_2^2 + ... + x_n^2 ≤ n.
If a player cannot choose a suitable x_n, the game ends and the other player wins. If the game goes on forever, neither player wins. All chosen numbers are known to both players.
Determine all values of λ for which Alice has a winning strategy and all those for which Bazza has a winning strategy."

Interpretation: The game proceeds in turns. On odd turns (n=1,3,5,...), Alice chooses x_n ≥ 0 subject to the constraint that the cumulative sum S_n = x_1+...+x_n ≤ λ n. On even turns (n=2,4,6,...), Bazza chooses x_n ≥ 0 subject to the constraint that the cumulative sum of squares Q_n = x_1^2+...+x_n^2 ≤ n. The game ends when a player cannot make a move (i.e., there is no nonnegative real number satisfying the inequality given the previous choices). That player loses, and the other wins. If the game continues indefinitely, it's a draw (neither wins). Both players know λ and all previous choices.

We need to find for which λ Alice has a winning strategy, and for which λ Bazza has a winning strategy. Possibly there is a threshold.

First, note that the constraints are cumulative: after each turn, the sum (for Alice's moves) must be ≤ λ n, and after each even turn, the sum of squares must be ≤ n. But these constraints are only applied at the moment the player chooses; the previous moves already satisfied constraints for previous n. So we have a sequence of constraints that must be maintained.

Let's denote n odd: Alice's turn. She picks x_n ≥ 0 such that S_n = S_{n-1} + x_n ≤ λ n. Since S_{n-1} already satisfied S_{n-1} ≤ λ (n-1) (from previous odd turn? Actually after n-1, if n-1 is even, then the constraint is on squares, not sum. But Alice's constraint only applies on odd turns; the sum constraint must hold at each odd n. However, there is no constraint on sum after even turns. So S_n is only required to satisfy S_n ≤ λ n when n is odd. But it could be that S_n > λ n for even n? That's allowed because the constraint is only checked at the end of Alice's turn. For even n, Bazza's constraint is on squares, not sum. So Alice's moves must ensure that after her turn, sum ≤ λ n. But she also must consider that future moves (Bazza's) will impose square constraints that might restrict her future choices indirectly because the squares accumulate and must stay ≤ n at even n. So both players' moves affect the other's future possibilities.

We need to analyze the game as a kind of "competitive" sequence. Typically such problems have a threshold λ0 such that if λ > λ0, Alice can force a win; if λ < λ0, Bazza can force a win; if λ = λ0, maybe draw or one wins? But the problem asks "determine all values of λ for which Alice has a winning strategy and all those for which Bazza has a winning strategy." So likely there is a range for each.

Let's think about the constraints.

Define cumulative sums: S_n = ∑_{i=1}^n x_i, Q_n = ∑_{i=1}^n x_i^2.

Constraints:
- For odd n: S_n ≤ λ n.
- For even n: Q_n ≤ n.

Players choose numbers nonnegative.

Goal: If at some turn, the player cannot choose any nonnegative number satisfying the constraint, they lose. So the game ends when the constraint is impossible to satisfy given previous numbers.

Note that the constraints are inequalities; the player can always choose 0, but does that satisfy the constraint? For Alice on odd n: she must pick x_n ≥ 0 such that S_{n-1}+x_n ≤ λ n. Since S_{n-1} is known, if S_{n-1} ≤ λ n, then she can choose x_n=0 and satisfy. But if S_{n-1} > λ n, then no nonnegative x_n can satisfy because even x_n=0 gives S_{n-1} > λ n. So Alice can only move if S_{n-1} ≤ λ n. Similarly, for Bazza on even n: he must pick x_n ≥ 0 such that Q_{n-1}+x_n^2 ≤ n. Since Q_{n-1} is known, if Q_{n-1} ≤ n, then he can choose x_n=0 and satisfy. If Q_{n-1} > n, then even x_n=0 gives Q_{n-1} > n, so no move. So Bazza can move iff Q_{n-1} ≤ n.

Thus the game continues as long as at each turn, the cumulative sum or sum of squares from previous moves is within the bound for that turn. But note: after Alice's move, the sum constraint is checked; but after Bazza's move, the square constraint is checked. However, the sum constraint might become violated after Bazza's move? That's allowed because the sum constraint is only required at odd turns. So the only condition for the game to continue is that at each odd n, S_{n-1} ≤ λ n, and at each even n, Q_{n-1} ≤ n. Since players can always choose 0 if the condition holds, the game will continue indefinitely if these conditions are always satisfied. The game ends when at some turn, the condition fails (i.e., S_{n-1} > λ n for odd n, or Q_{n-1} > n for even n). Then the player whose turn it is cannot move, so loses.

Thus the game reduces to a "survival" game: both players try to avoid being the one who cannot move. Alice wants to force Bazza into a position where at an even turn, Q_{n-1} > n, so Bazza cannot move; Bazza wants to force Alice into a position where at an odd turn, S_{n-1} > λ n, so Alice cannot move. Also note that if the game goes on forever, neither wins; but if one player can force a win, that's the outcome.

We need to determine for which λ Alice can force a win, i.e., she can make moves such that eventually Bazza has no legal move, and she can avoid being forced into a position where she cannot move. Similarly for Bazza.

Since both players have perfect information, this is a deterministic game with infinite horizon. We can try to analyze the "value" of the game in terms of the constraints.

Let’s denote the state after some number of moves. At each stage, we have S_n and Q_n (but note that S_n and Q_n are not independent; they are linked by the x_i's). The players can influence the future by choosing numbers. The game is essentially about controlling the sums.

Observe that for any sequence of nonnegative numbers, by Cauchy-Schwarz, we have S_n^2 ≤ n Q_n. So Q_n ≥ S_n^2 / n. Also, S_n ≤ λ n? But that's only required at odd n. So we have a relationship between S and Q.

For Bazza's constraint: at even n, we need Q_n ≤ n. Since Q_n ≥ S_n^2 / n, this implies S_n^2 / n ≤ Q_n ≤ n, so S_n^2 ≤ n^2, i.e., S_n ≤ n. So from Bazza's constraint, we get that at even n, S_n ≤ n (since all x_i nonnegative, S_n is nondecreasing; but we need to be careful: the condition Q_n ≤ n is required at even n, but that condition doesn't directly impose S_n ≤ n; it's a consequence of Cauchy-Schwarz: S_n^2 ≤ n Q_n ≤ n^2, so S_n ≤ n. So indeed, if Bazza can move (i.e., Q_{n-1} ≤ n), then after his move, we have Q_n ≤ n, so S_n ≤ n. So the sum is bounded by n at even steps. Similarly, at odd steps, we have S_n ≤ λ n.

But note: the sum S_n is not necessarily bounded by n at odd n; it's bounded by λ n. So λ could be larger than 1. But if λ > 1, then Alice's constraint is looser; she can allow larger sums. However, Bazza's constraint forces the sum to be at most n at even steps. So if λ is very large, Alice could pick large numbers, but then at the next even step, Bazza might be forced to keep squares low; but the sum could become large, but that's okay for Bazza's constraint? Actually Bazza's constraint is on squares, not sum. But if the sum becomes too large, the square sum might become too large, because large numbers increase squares quickly. So there's a tradeoff.

We can think of the game as a "resource" game: each player chooses numbers that affect cumulative sums. Perhaps we can find a strategy for Alice to force a win if λ > 1? Or maybe if λ > something like 2? Let's test small values.

We need to consider the possibility of drawing (infinite play). For the game to go on forever, both players must always be able to move. That means that at each odd n, S_{n-1} ≤ λ n, and at each even n, Q_{n-1} ≤ n. Since players can always choose 0, the only way the game ends is if a constraint becomes impossible to satisfy. So for infinite play, we need that these constraints are always satisfied. But can the game go on forever for any λ? Possibly not; if λ is too small, Bazza might force Alice to exceed the sum bound, or vice versa. We need to determine winning strategies.

Let’s analyze the game more systematically.

Define the state as (S, Q, n) where n is the number of moves made so far, but the next turn depends on parity. Since the game is infinite horizon, we can consider the "value" of the game from the start. But we can also consider the possibility of "strategy stealing" or "pairing" strategies.

We can try to see if Bazza has a winning strategy for λ ≤ some threshold. Since Bazza's constraint is on squares, he might try to keep the sum of squares low, which limits how large the sum can become. For Alice, she wants to force Bazza into a situation where Q_{n-1} > n, i.e., the sum of squares is already too high for Bazza to add any number. Since Bazza can always choose 0, he only fails if Q_{n-1} > n. So Alice wants to make the sum of squares exceed n at an even step before Bazza's move. But Bazza's moves affect Q, so Alice must consider that Bazza might try to keep Q low.

Similarly, Bazza wants to make S_{n-1} > λ n at an odd step before Alice's move.

Thus it's a kind of "pursuit" where each player tries to push the opponent's cumulative measure beyond a bound.

Let’s denote n. We'll consider the game from the start with no moves. At n=1 (odd), Alice chooses x_1 ≥ 0 such that x_1 ≤ λ*1 = λ. She can choose any x_1 in [0, λ]. She might want to set x_1 to something that makes it hard for Bazza later. Since at n=2 (even), Bazza must choose x_2 ≥ 0 such that x_1^2 + x_2^2 ≤ 2. So Bazza can always choose 0 if x_1^2 ≤ 2. If x_1^2 > 2, then Bazza cannot move? Actually if x_1^2 > 2, then even with x_2=0, the sum of squares is >2, so Bazza loses immediately (cannot choose any x_2). So if λ > sqrt(2), Alice could choose x_1 > sqrt(2), and then Bazza would have no legal move, so Alice wins. So for λ > sqrt(2), Alice has a winning strategy: pick x_1 = sqrt(2)+ε (but ≤ λ). Since λ > sqrt(2), she can pick any number > sqrt(2) and ≤ λ. However, is it always possible? She must satisfy x_1 ≤ λ, so if λ > sqrt(2), she can pick x_1 = sqrt(2)+δ for some small δ. That gives x_1^2 = 2 + 2δ sqrt(2)+δ^2 > 2, so Bazza loses at n=2. So Alice wins. So for λ > sqrt(2), Alice wins.

What about λ = sqrt(2)? She can pick x_1 ≤ sqrt(2). If she picks x_1 = sqrt(2), then x_1^2 = 2, so at n=2, Bazza needs x_2 such that 2 + x_2^2 ≤ 2, so x_2=0 is allowed. So Bazza can move. If she picks x_1 < sqrt(2), then Bazza can move. So no immediate win. But maybe Alice can still force a win later? We need to analyze λ ≤ sqrt(2). For λ < sqrt(2), she cannot pick x_1 > sqrt(2), so Bazza will always have at least the move 0. So the game might continue. For λ = sqrt(2), she can pick x_1 = sqrt(2) and then Bazza can choose 0, but maybe she can still force a win? Possibly not, because Bazza might have a strategy to keep the sum of squares low and sum bounded. We'll need to analyze the range λ ≤ sqrt(2) more deeply.

Now, for λ ≤ sqrt(2), Alice cannot win immediately. Could Bazza have a winning strategy? For Bazza to win, he must force Alice into a situation where at an odd n, S_{n-1} > λ n. Since Alice can always choose 0, she can avoid that if possible. So Bazza wants to make the cumulative sum exceed λ n at an odd step before Alice's move. But note that Alice chooses x_n on odd turns, so the sum after her move is S_n ≤ λ n. So the only way to force Alice to lose is to have S_{n-1} > λ n, i.e., before her move, the sum from previous moves already exceeds λ n. Since she only adds nonnegative numbers, the sum S_{n-1} is the sum of all previous x_i, which includes both her previous moves and Bazza's moves. So Bazza wants to make the sum large enough that at some odd n, S_{n-1} > λ n, so Alice cannot move. But note that after Bazza's move at even n, the sum is S_n. Then the next turn is odd n+1, so Alice will need to have S_n ≤ λ (n+1). Since S_n is the sum after Bazza's move, it could be large. But if λ is small, maybe Bazza can force the sum to grow quickly? However, Bazza's moves are constrained by the square sum. He can choose numbers to increase the sum without increasing squares too much? He can choose small numbers; but to increase the sum, he might need to pick larger numbers, which increases squares. So he must balance.

We can try to find a threshold λ0 such that if λ > λ0, Alice wins; if λ < λ0, Bazza wins; and at λ = λ0, maybe draw? But we already have λ > sqrt(2) gives Alice win. So if the threshold is less than sqrt(2), then for λ in (threshold, sqrt(2)] maybe Alice wins? But we already found that at λ = sqrt(2), Alice cannot win immediately. Could she still win? Let's explore.

We need to determine the precise winning conditions.

Let's try to analyze the game as a kind of "competitive" sequence where the players try to keep the sum of squares below n at even steps and sum below λ n at odd steps. This resembles a game where one player controls the sum and the other controls the sum of squares. It might be related to the inequality S_n^2 ≤ n Q_n. So at any n, we have Q_n ≥ S_n^2 / n. So if Bazza can force that Q_n is always ≤ n, then S_n ≤ n. So the sum is bounded by n at even steps. But at odd steps, we have S_n ≤ λ n. So if λ > 1, then Alice's bound is larger than the implied bound from Bazza's constraint at even steps. But Bazza can try to keep Q low to restrict S. However, if λ is small, say λ < 1, then Alice's constraint is tighter, and Bazza might be able to push the sum to exceed λ n at some odd step? Let's think.

We need to consider the game from both perspectives. Let's denote the "potential" of the game.

One typical approach: Consider the worst-case scenario for each player. For Alice to have a winning strategy, she can force Bazza to eventually be unable to move. This means she can choose numbers such that at some even n, Q_{n-1} > n, so Bazza cannot choose any x_n (including 0). Since Bazza can always choose 0 if Q_{n-1} ≤ n, the only way to force Bazza to lose is to make Q_{n-1} > n. So Alice wants to make the sum of squares exceed n before Bazza's turn. But Bazza also chooses numbers, so he can try to keep Q low. However, Alice can choose large numbers; but if she chooses too large, then the sum constraint might be violated at her own turn? Actually her constraint is on sum, not squares. So she can choose large numbers as long as the sum after her move does not exceed λ n. So if λ is large, she can pick huge numbers, but then the square sum might become large, which might be okay for her? Actually the square sum is the sum of squares; if she picks a large number, the square is huge, and that could cause Q to exceed n at some even step, which is good for her (forces Bazza to lose). But careful: She picks x_n on odd n; after her move, Q_n is increased by x_n^2. Then the next turn is even n+1, and Bazza must choose x_{n+1} such that Q_n + x_{n+1}^2 ≤ n+1. If Q_n > n+1, then Bazza cannot even choose 0, so he loses immediately. So if Alice can make Q_n > n+1 on her turn (i.e., after her move), then Bazza loses at the next even turn. So Alice's winning condition is to achieve Q_n > n+1 at an odd n. But note that Q_n is the sum of squares after her move. Since n is odd, the bound for Q at the next even step is n+1. So if she can make Q_n > n+1, then Bazza cannot move. So she wants to push Q_n above the next even bound.

Similarly, Bazza wants to make S_n > λ (n+1) at an even n, so that at the next odd turn, Alice cannot move. Because after his move, S_n is the sum after his move; then at odd n+1, Alice needs S_n ≤ λ (n+1). If S_n > λ (n+1), she loses.

Thus the game can be seen as: At each odd step n, Alice can choose x_n to increase S_n and Q_n, but must keep S_n ≤ λ n. At each even step n, Bazza chooses x_n to increase S_n and Q_n, but must keep Q_n ≤ n. The game ends if at the start of a turn, the opponent's constraint is violated (i.e., before the player chooses, the cumulative sum or sum of squares is already beyond the bound for that turn). So it's like a "race" where each player tries to make the opponent's cumulative measure exceed a certain bound.

Now, we can try to analyze the "value" of the game in terms of the maximum possible sum or square sum that can be forced.

We need a rigorous analysis of the game. Let's define the game state more abstractly.

We have two sequences: x_n. The constraints are:
- After each odd n: S_n ≤ λ n.
- After each even n: Q_n ≤ n.

Players choose numbers. The game ends when at the start of a turn, the constraint for that turn is impossible to satisfy (i.e., the cumulative sum or sum of squares already exceeds the bound). So if at odd n, S_{n-1} > λ n, Alice loses; if at even n, Q_{n-1} > n, Bazza loses.

Thus we can think of the game as two players trying to push the opponent's cumulative measure beyond a bound. The key is the relationship between S and Q via Cauchy-Schwarz: Q_n ≥ S_n^2 / n. So if Bazza wants to make Q_n exceed n (to force Alice to lose at odd n+1?), actually Bazza loses if Q_{n-1} > n at even n. So Alice wants to make Q_{n-1} > n. But Q_{n-1} ≥ S_{n-1}^2 / (n-1). So if S_{n-1} is large enough, then Q_{n-1} might exceed n. But that's the condition for Alice to win: at an odd n, after her move, Q_n > n+1? Actually careful: Alice wins if at some even n, Q_{n-1} > n. Since she chooses x_n on odd n, after her move, Q_n is increased. Then at even n+1, Bazza must have Q_n ≤ n+1. So if Q_n > n+1, Bazza loses. So Alice wants to make Q_n > n+1 at an odd n. Since Q_n = Q_{n-1} + x_n^2, and she can choose x_n up to λ n - S_{n-1}. So she wants to maximize x_n^2 given S_{n-1} and the bound. So the condition for her to be able to win on her turn is: there exists x_n ≥ 0 such that x_n ≤ λ n - S_{n-1} and Q_{n-1} + x_n^2 > n+1. Since she can choose x_n, she can achieve that if and only if Q_{n-1} + (λ n - S_{n-1})^2 > n+1, provided λ n - S_{n-1} ≥ 0. If λ n - S_{n-1} < 0, she cannot even move. So Alice has a winning strategy if she can force the state to such that at some odd n, Q_{n-1} + (λ n - S_{n-1})^2 > n+1 and also S_{n-1} ≤ λ n. But note: if she can achieve that, she can win by choosing x_n = λ n - S_{n-1} (or maybe a slightly smaller if that makes the square exceed the bound? Actually she wants to make Q_n > n+1, so she can pick the maximum possible x_n to maximize x_n^2. So if that maximum yields Q_n > n+1, she wins. So the condition for her to be able to win on her turn is: Q_{n-1} + (λ n - S_{n-1})^2 > n+1. But she also must have S_{n-1} ≤ λ n to be able to move (otherwise she loses immediately). So if the state (S_{n-1}, Q_{n-1}) satisfies S_{n-1} ≤ λ n and Q_{n-1} + (λ n - S_{n-1})^2 > n+1, then Alice can win by playing x_n = λ n - S_{n-1}. However, note that she could also play a smaller x_n; but the maximum gives the best chance. So she will win if she can achieve that condition.

Similarly, Bazza wants to make S_{n-1} > λ n at an even n. Since after his move at even n, S_n = S_{n-1} + x_n. He can choose x_n up to sqrt(n - Q_{n-1}). He wants to make S_n > λ (n+1)? Actually he wants at the next odd turn n+1, Alice to lose: S_n > λ (n+1). Since he chooses x_n, he can increase S. So his winning condition at even n is: there exists x_n ≥ 0 such that x_n ≤ sqrt(n - Q_{n-1}) and S_{n-1} + x_n > λ (n+1). Since he can choose x_n, he can achieve that if and only if S_{n-1} + sqrt(n - Q_{n-1}) > λ (n+1), provided sqrt(n - Q_{n-1}) ≥ 0. Also, he must have Q_{n-1} ≤ n to be able to move (otherwise he loses immediately). So if the state (S_{n-1}, Q_{n-1}) satisfies Q_{n-1} ≤ n and S_{n-1} + sqrt(n - Q_{n-1}) > λ (n+1), then Bazza can win by playing x_n = sqrt(n - Q_{n-1}) (or maybe a smaller if that makes the sum exceed the bound? Actually he wants to maximize S, so he picks the maximum possible x_n). So if that condition holds, Bazza can win.

Thus the game is about the players trying to steer the state to satisfy their winning condition. The game could also be drawn if the players can avoid ever satisfying the opponent's winning condition. But note that if one player can force a win, they will eventually do so.

We can try to find the "critical" region in the (S, Q) plane for the game. The initial state: after 0 moves, S_0=0, Q_0=0. The first turn is odd (n=1). Alice must choose x_1 such that S_1 ≤ λ*1. She can pick any x_1 in [0, λ]. So after her move, state (S_1, Q_1) = (x_1, x_1^2). She might want to set x_1 to something that gives her a chance to win later. For her to win, she needs at some later odd n, Q_{n-1} + (λ n - S_{n-1})^2 > n+1. Since she can control x_1, she can try to set up the state.

For Bazza to win, he needs at some even n, S_{n-1} + sqrt(n - Q_{n-1}) > λ (n+1). He can choose x_n.

We need to determine for which λ Alice has a winning strategy. That is, can she force the state to satisfy her winning condition before Bazza can force his? Since both players are rational, we can analyze the game as a kind of "reachability" game.

Let’s try to find a threshold λ0 such that:
- If λ > λ0, Alice can force a win.
- If λ < λ0, Bazza can force a win.
- At λ = λ0, maybe draw or one wins? Typically such problems have a threshold where one player can force a win for larger λ, and the other for smaller λ, and at the threshold perhaps the game is a draw (both can avoid losing indefinitely) or maybe one still wins. We need to find all λ for which Alice has a winning strategy, and all those for which Bazza has a winning strategy. Possibly there is a range for each, with a gap (draw) or overlap? Typically in such two-player games with cumulative constraints, the threshold is λ0 = 1? Or maybe λ0 = √2? We already saw λ > √2 gives Alice immediate win. But for λ=√2, she also can win (by playing x_1=√2, then x_3 > √2). So that is above the threshold? Actually we need to find the exact set.

Let's try to analyze the game more systematically. The game is similar to a "duel" where each player chooses a number that influences the next bound. There is a known inequality: For any sequence, we have S_n^2 ≤ n Q_n. So at any n, we have S_n^2 ≤ n Q_n. Since the constraints are S_n ≤ λ n at odd n and Q_n ≤ n at even n, we have some constraints.

Suppose we consider the "worst-case" scenario for Alice if she wants to avoid losing. She might try to keep S low and Q low? But that might give Bazza room to increase S. She wants to force Q to exceed the next even bound. She can do that by picking a large x at an odd step, but that will increase S as well, which might help Bazza later? Actually if she picks a large x, she increases both S and Q. The increase in S might be a problem for her if Bazza later pushes the sum over the bound. But she can also choose x to be something that makes Q exceed the bound, which is her goal. So she might want to use her allowed sum to increase Q as much as possible. Since the sum constraint is linear, she can choose x_n to be as large as possible given S_{n-1}. To maximize Q increase, she would choose the maximum allowed x_n, which is λ n - S_{n-1}. So if she always uses the maximum at each odd step, she maximizes Q and also S. But then at the next even step, Bazza's bound might be tight. However, we need to see if she can force Q_n > n+1 at some odd n. Since she controls x_n, she can try to achieve that condition. But she also must consider that Bazza might choose numbers that affect S_{n-1} and Q_{n-1}. So she cannot simply assume she will be able to pick the maximum; Bazza might restrict her by making S_{n-1} large? But she can choose x_n to be something; if S_{n-1} is large, then her allowed x_n is small, which may not allow her to exceed the bound. So she wants to keep S_{n-1} small to have room for a large x_n. So she might try to keep S low by picking 0 at odd steps? But if she picks 0, she doesn't increase Q much, so she may not be able to push Q over the bound. So she needs to find a balance.

We need to see if there exists a strategy for Bazza that guarantees that Alice never gets a chance to make Q exceed the bound. That is, he wants to keep the state (S, Q) such that at the next odd step, Alice's allowed x is not too large, and also the square sum is not too high. This is a game of "survival" for Bazza. He can choose x_2. The optimal play for Bazza is to choose x_2 to minimize Alice's chance to win. So we need to find, for a given λ, whether Bazza can always respond to keep Alice from winning. This is like a "reachability" game. We can try to analyze the "critical" curve where Alice can force a win. The threshold λ0 might be where the "maximal" strategy for Bazza changes from being a winning strategy to a drawing strategy. For λ=0.95, we saw that if Bazza chooses x_2=1.047 (max), then Alice can win by playing x_3=0 and then later winning. But if he chooses x_2=0.5, then she might not win. So he can choose a smaller x_2 to survive. So we need to see if for λ=0.95, there exists a strategy for Bazza such that no matter how Alice plays, she cannot win. That would require that for every possible x_1 (which she chooses first), Bazza can choose x_2 to limit her. But she chooses x_1 first. So we need to consider the game from the start. The first move is Alice's. She can choose any x_1 in [0, λ]. She wants to eventually win. She can choose x_1 to maximize her chances. So we need to see if there exists a strategy for her such that for any response by Bazza, she can force a win. If yes, she has a winning strategy. If not, then Bazza might have a drawing strategy. So the problem is to determine the set of λ for which Alice has a winning strategy (i.e., she can force a win no matter what Bazza does), and the set for which Bazza has a winning strategy (i.e., he can force a win no matter what Alice does). So pattern: After the initial few steps, the sequence seems to settle into a pattern where the allowed x at odd steps is about 0.324 (or 1.324, 0.703, etc.) but the sum S seems to be increasing. In this play, we saw that at n=79, the allowed x was 0.448, and she picked that, but then later she got a chance to pick 1.324 at n=71? Actually we had a large allowed x at n=71, n=73, n=91, etc. The key is that she eventually gets a large allowed x at some odd step that allows her to make Q exceed the bound. In the above, after n=80, she had Q_80=80.0, S_80=68.491, then at n=81 she picked 0.359, etc. It seems she might be able to win eventually. But we need to see if she can force a win for λ=0.85. In the above, we didn't see a win yet; we computed up to n=100 and still no win. It might be that for λ=0.85, the game is a draw as well. However, we saw that for λ=0.85, if she picks x_5=0, then the allowed x at n=7 is 1.142, and she could pick 1.5? But that gave a win? Let's recalc that play for λ=0.85 with x_5=0.
